search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other people named John Milton, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/John_Milton_(disambiguation)" title="John Milton (disambiguation)">John Milton (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">17th-century English poet and civil servant</div>\n<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold"><div class="fn" style="display:inline;">John Milton</div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" London, England</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Resting place</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;"><a style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Occupation</th><td class="role" style="line-height:1.4em;">Poet, <a href="/wiki/Prose" title="Prose">prose</a> <a style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Language</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;">English, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" Cambridge">Christ\'s College, Cambridge</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;"><hr/></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Signature</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Milton_signature.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="135" data-file-width="585" decoding="async" height="37" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/John_Milton_signature.svg/160px-John_Milton_signature.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/John_Milton_signature.svg/240px-John_Milton_signature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/John_Milton_signature.svg/320px-John_Milton_signature.svg.png 2x" width="160"/></a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>John Milton</b> (9 December 1608&#160;&#8211; 8 November 1674) was an English poet, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polemicist" title="Polemicist">polemicist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man_of_letters" title="Man of letters">man of letters</a>, and civil servant for the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a> under its Council of State and later under <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>. He wrote at a time of religious flux and political upheaval, and is best known for his <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic poem</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost">Paradise Lost</a></i> (1667), written in <a href="/wiki/Blank_verse" title="Blank verse">blank verse</a>.\n</p><p>Writing in English, Latin, Greek, and Italian, he achieved international renown within his lifetime, and his celebrated <i><a href="/wiki/Areopagitica" title="Areopagitica">Areopagitica</a></i> (1644), written in condemnation of <a href="/wiki/Prior_restraint" title="Prior restraint">pre-publication censorship</a>, is among history\'s most influential and impassioned defences of <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press" title="Freedom of the press">freedom of the press</a>. His desire for freedom extended into his style: he introduced new words (coined from Latin) to the English language, and was the first modern writer to employ non-rhymed verse outside of the theatre or translations.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_Hayley" title="William Hayley">William Hayley</a>\'s 1796 biography called him the "greatest English author",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> and he remains generally regarded "as one of the preeminent writers in the English language",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> though critical reception has oscillated in the centuries since his death (often on account of his <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a>). <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a> praised <i>Paradise Lost</i> as "a poem which...with respect to design may claim the first place, and with respect to performance, the second, among the productions of the human mind", though he (a <a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">Tory</a> and recipient of royal patronage) described Milton\'s politics as those of an "acrimonious and surly republican".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Works_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Works-3">[3]</a></sup> Poets such as <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hardy" title="Thomas Hardy">Thomas Hardy</a> revered him.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22John+Milton%22" rel="nofollow">"John Milton"</a>&#160;&#8211;&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22John+Milton%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">news</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22John+Milton%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22John+Milton%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22John+Milton%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22John+Milton%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">April 2017</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The phases of Milton\'s life parallel the major historical and political divisions in <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">Stuart</a> Britain. Milton studied, travelled, wrote poetry mostly for private circulation, and launched a career as pamphleteer and publicist under the increasingly personal rule of <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a> and its breakdown into constitutional confusion and war. The shift in accepted attitudes in government placed him in public office under the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a>, from being thought dangerously radical and even heretical, and he even acted as an official spokesman in certain of his publications. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Restoration" title="English Restoration">Restoration</a> of 1660 deprived Milton, now completely blind, of his public platform, but this period saw him complete most of his major works of poetry.\n</p><p>Milton\'s views developed from his very extensive reading, as well as travel and experience, from his student days of the 1620s to the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> By the time of his death in 1674, Milton was impoverished and on the margins of English intellectual life, yet famous throughout Europe and unrepentant for his political choices.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Milton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_of_John_Milton" title="Early life of John Milton">Early life of John Milton</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Milton_plaque_Bread_Street_London.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="823" data-file-width="1118" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/John_Milton_plaque_Bread_Street_London.jpg/220px-John_Milton_plaque_Bread_Street_London.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/John_Milton_plaque_Bread_Street_London.jpg/330px-John_Milton_plaque_Bread_Street_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/John_Milton_plaque_Bread_Street_London.jpg/440px-John_Milton_plaque_Bread_Street_London.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Milton_plaque_Bread_Street_London.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Blue plaque in Bread Street, London, where Milton was born</div></div></div>\n<p>John Milton was born in Bread Street, London on 9 December 1608, the son of composer <a href="/wiki/John_Milton_(composer)" title="John Milton (composer)">John Milton</a> and his wife Sarah Jeffrey. The senior John Milton (1562&#8211;1647) moved to London around 1583 after being disinherited by his devout Catholic father Richard "the Ranger" Milton for embracing Protestantism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-5">[5]</a></sup> In London, the senior John Milton married Sarah Jeffrey (1572&#8211;1637) and found lasting financial success as a <a href="/wiki/Scrivener" title="Scrivener">scrivener</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forsyth16_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forsyth16-6">[6]</a></sup> He lived in and worked from a house on <a href="/wiki/Bread_Street" title="Bread Street">Bread Street</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Mermaid_Tavern" title="Mermaid Tavern">Mermaid Tavern</a> was located in <a href="/wiki/Cheapside" title="Cheapside">Cheapside</a>. The elder Milton was noted for his skill as a musical composer, and this talent left his son with a lifelong appreciation for music and friendships with musicians such as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Lawes" title="Henry Lawes">Henry Lawes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Milton\'s father\'s prosperity provided his eldest son with a private tutor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Young_(1587%E2%80%931655)" title="Thomas Young (1587&#8211;1655)">Thomas Young</a>, a Scottish Presbyterian with an M.A. from the University of St. Andrews. Research suggests that Young\'s influence served as the poet\'s introduction to religious radicalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> After Young\'s tutorship, Milton attended <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_School_(London)" title="St Paul\'s School (London)">St Paul\'s School</a> in London. There he began the study of Latin and Greek, and the classical languages left an imprint on both his poetry and prose in English (he also wrote in Italian and Latin).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Milton_portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="685" data-file-width="478" decoding="async" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/John_Milton_portrait.jpg/220px-John_Milton_portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/John_Milton_portrait.jpg/330px-John_Milton_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/John_Milton_portrait.jpg/440px-John_Milton_portrait.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Milton_portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Milton at age 10 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cornelis_Janssens_van_Ceulen" title="Cornelis Janssens van Ceulen">Cornelis Janssens van Ceulen</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Milton%27s_Cottage" title="Milton\'s Cottage">Milton\'s Cottage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chalfont_St_Giles" title="Chalfont St Giles">Chalfont St Giles</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Milton\'s first datable compositions are two psalms done at age 15 at <a href="/wiki/Long_Bennington" title="Long Bennington">Long Bennington</a>. One contemporary source is the <i><a href="/wiki/Brief_Lives" title="Brief Lives">Brief Lives</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/John_Aubrey" title="John Aubrey">John Aubrey</a>, an uneven compilation including first-hand reports. In the work, Aubrey quotes Christopher, Milton\'s younger brother: "When he was young, he studied very hard and sat up very late, commonly till twelve or one o\'clock at night". Aubrey adds, ""His complexion exceeding faire&#8212;he was so faire that they called him the Lady of Christ\'s College."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OLD_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-OLD-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1625, Milton began attending <a href="/wiki/Christ%27s_College,_Cambridge" title="Christ\'s College, Cambridge">Christ\'s College, Cambridge</a>. He graduated with a B.A. in 1629,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> ranking fourth of 24&#160;honours graduates that year in the University of Cambridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Preparing to become an <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a> priest, Milton stayed on and obtained his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Master_of_Arts_(Oxbridge_and_Dublin)" title="Master of Arts (Oxbridge and Dublin)">Master of Arts degree</a> on 3 July 1632.\n</p><p>Milton may have been <a href="/wiki/Rustication_(academia)" title="Rustication (academia)">rusticated</a> (suspended) in his first year for quarrelling with his tutor, Bishop <a href="/wiki/William_Chappell_(bishop)" title="William Chappell (bishop)">William Chappell</a>. He was certainly at home in London in the Lent Term 1626; there he wrote his <i>Elegia Prima</i>, a first Latin <a href="/wiki/Elegy" title="Elegy">elegy</a>, to Charles Diodati, a friend from St Paul\'s. Based on remarks of <a href="/wiki/John_Aubrey" title="John Aubrey">John Aubrey</a>, Chappell "whipt" Milton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OLD_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-OLD-9">[9]</a></sup> This story is now disputed, though certainly Milton disliked Chappell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hill_(historian)" title="Christopher Hill (historian)">Christopher Hill</a> cautiously notes that Milton was "apparently" rusticated, and that the differences between Chappell and Milton may have been either religious or personal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> It is also possible that, like <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> four decades later, Milton was sent home because of the <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plague</a>, by which Cambridge was badly affected in 1625. In 1626, Milton\'s tutor was Nathaniel Tovey.\n</p><p>At Cambridge, Milton was on good terms with <a href="/wiki/Edward_King_(British_poet)" title="Edward King (British poet)">Edward King</a>, for whom he later wrote "<a href="/wiki/Lycidas" title="Lycidas">Lycidas</a>". He also befriended Anglo-American dissident and theologian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roger_Williams_(theologian)" title="Roger Williams (theologian)">Roger Williams</a>. Milton tutored Williams in Hebrew in exchange for lessons in Dutch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Despite developing a reputation for poetic skill and general erudition, Milton experienced alienation from his peers and university life as a whole. Having once watched his fellow students attempting comedy upon the college stage, he later observed \'they thought themselves gallant men, and I thought them fools\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Milton was disdainful of the university curriculum, which consisted of stilted formal debates conducted in Latin on abstruse topics. His own corpus is not devoid of humour, notably his sixth <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/prolusion" title="wikt:prolusion">prolusion</a> and his epitaphs on the death of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobson" title="Thomas Hobson">Thomas Hobson</a>. While at college, he wrote a number of his well-known shorter English poems, among them "On the Morning of Christ\'s Nativity", his "Epitaph on the admirable Dramaticke Poet, W. Shakespeare" (his first poem to appear in print), <i><a href="/wiki/L%27Allegro" title="L\'Allegro">L\'Allegro</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Il_Penseroso" title="Il Penseroso">Il Penseroso</a></i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Study.2C_poetry.2C_and_travel"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Study,_poetry,_and_travel">Study, poetry, and travel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Milton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Study, poetry, and travel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_of_John_Milton" title="Early life of John Milton">Early life of John Milton</a></div>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:40%; ;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8220; ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8221; ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">It appears in all his writings that he had the usual concomitant of great abilities, a lofty and steady confidence in himself, perhaps not without some contempt of others; for scarcely any man ever wrote so much, and praised so few. Of his praise he was very frugal; as he set its value high, and considered his mention of a name as a security against the waste of time, and a certain preservative from oblivion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">&#8211; <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lives_of_the_Most_Eminent_English_Poets" title="Lives of the Most Eminent English Poets">Lives of the Most Eminent English Poets</a></i></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Upon receiving his M.A. in 1632, Milton retired to <a href="/wiki/Hammersmith" title="Hammersmith">Hammersmith</a>, his father\'s new home since the previous year. He also lived at <a href="/wiki/Horton,_Berkshire" title="Horton, Berkshire">Horton</a>, Berkshire, from 1635 and undertook six years of self-directed private study. Hill argues that this was not retreat into a rural idyll; Hammersmith was then a "suburban village" falling into the orbit of London, and even Horton was becoming deforested and suffered from the plague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> He read both ancient and modern works of theology, philosophy, history, politics, literature, and science in preparation for a prospective poetical career. Milton\'s intellectual development can be charted via entries in his <a href="/wiki/Commonplace_book" title="Commonplace book">commonplace book</a> (like a scrapbook), now in the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a>. As a result of such intensive study, Milton is considered to be among the most learned of all English poets. In addition to his years of private study, Milton had command of Latin, Greek, Hebrew, French, Spanish, and Italian from his school and undergraduate days; he also added Old English to his linguistic repertoire in the 1650s while researching his <i>History of Britain</i>, and probably acquired proficiency in Dutch soon after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Milton_plaque_Berkyn_Manor_Farm_Horton.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/John_Milton_plaque_Berkyn_Manor_Farm_Horton.JPG/170px-John_Milton_plaque_Berkyn_Manor_Farm_Horton.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/John_Milton_plaque_Berkyn_Manor_Farm_Horton.JPG/255px-John_Milton_plaque_Berkyn_Manor_Farm_Horton.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/John_Milton_plaque_Berkyn_Manor_Farm_Horton.JPG/340px-John_Milton_plaque_Berkyn_Manor_Farm_Horton.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Milton_plaque_Berkyn_Manor_Farm_Horton.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Commemorative blue plaque \'John Milton lived here 1632&#8211;1638\' at Berkyn Manor Farm, <a href="/wiki/Horton,_Berkshire" title="Horton, Berkshire">Horton, Berkshire</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Milton continued to write poetry during this period of study; his <i><a href="/wiki/Arcades_(Milton)" title="Arcades (Milton)">Arcades</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comus_(John_Milton)" title="Comus (John Milton)">Comus</a></i> were both commissioned for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masques" title="Masques">masques</a> composed for noble patrons, connections of the Egerton family, and performed in 1632 and 1634 respectively. <i>Comus</i> argues for the virtuousness of <a href="/wiki/Temperance_(virtue)" title="Temperance (virtue)">temperance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chastity" title="Chastity">chastity</a>.  He contributed his <a href="/wiki/Pastoral" title="Pastoral">pastoral</a> <a href="/wiki/Elegy" title="Elegy">elegy</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Lycidas" title="Lycidas">Lycidas</a></i> to a memorial collection for one of his fellow-students at Cambridge.  Drafts of these poems are preserved in Milton\'s poetry notebook, known as the Trinity Manuscript because it is now kept at <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College</a>, Cambridge.\n</p><p>In May 1638, Milton embarked upon a tour of France and Italy that lasted until July or August 1639.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> His travels supplemented his study with new and direct experience of artistic and religious traditions, especially Roman Catholicism. He met famous theorists and intellectuals of the time, and was able to display his poetic skills. For specific details of what happened within Milton\'s "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_tour" title="Grand tour">grand tour</a>", there appears to be just one <a href="/wiki/Primary_source" title="Primary source">primary source</a>: Milton\'s own <i><a href="/wiki/Defensio_Secunda" title="Defensio Secunda">Defensio Secunda</a></i>. There are other records, including some letters and some references in his other prose tracts, but the bulk of the information about the tour comes from a work that, according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barbara_Lewalski" title="Barbara Lewalski">Barbara Lewalski</a>, "was not intended as autobiography but as rhetoric, designed to emphasise his sterling reputation with the learned of Europe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He first went to <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">Calais</a> and then on to Paris, riding horseback, with a letter from diplomat <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wotton" title="Henry Wotton">Henry Wotton</a> to ambassador <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Scudamore_(ambassador)" title="John Scudamore (ambassador)">John Scudamore</a>. Through Scudamore, Milton met <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a>, a Dutch law philosopher, playwright, and poet. Milton left France soon after this meeting. He travelled south from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nice,_France" title="Nice, France">Nice</a> to <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>, and then to <a href="/wiki/Livorno" title="Livorno">Livorno</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">Pisa</a>. He reached Florence in July 1638. While there, Milton enjoyed many of the sites and structures of the city. His candour of manner and erudite neo-Latin poetry earned him friends in Florentine intellectual circles, and he met the astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo" title="Galileo">Galileo</a> who was under <a href="/wiki/House_arrest" title="House arrest">house arrest</a> at <a href="/wiki/Arcetri" title="Arcetri">Arcetri</a>, as well as others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Milton probably visited the Florentine Academy and the <a href="/wiki/Accademia_della_Crusca" title="Accademia della Crusca">Accademia della Crusca</a> along with smaller academies in the area, including the Apatisti and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Svogliati" title="Svogliati">Svogliati</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:40%; ;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303512" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">In [Florence], which I have always admired above all others because of the elegance, not just of its tongue, but also of its wit, I lingered for about two months. There I at once became the friend of many gentlemen eminent in rank and learning, whose private academies I frequented&#8212;a Florentine institution which deserves great praise not only for promoting humane studies but also for encouraging friendly intercourse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">&#8211; Milton\'s account of Florence in <i>Defensio Secunda</i></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>He left Florence in September to continue to Rome. With the connections from Florence, Milton was able to have easy access to Rome\'s intellectual society. His poetic abilities impressed those like Giovanni Salzilli, who praised Milton within an epigram. In late October, Milton attended a dinner given by the <a href="/wiki/English_College,_Rome" title="English College, Rome">English College, Rome</a>, despite his dislike for the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Society of Jesus</a>, meeting English Catholics who were also guests&#8212;theologian <a href="/wiki/Henry_Holden" title="Henry Holden">Henry Holden</a> and the poet <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Cary" title="Patrick Cary">Patrick Cary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> He also attended musical events, including oratorios, operas, and melodramas. Milton left for <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a> toward the end of November, where he stayed only for a month because of the Spanish control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> During that time, he was introduced to Giovanni Battista Manso, patron to both <a href="/wiki/Torquato_Tasso" title="Torquato Tasso">Torquato Tasso</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Giambattista_Marino" title="Giambattista Marino">Giambattista Marino</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Originally, Milton wanted to leave Naples in order to travel to <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and then on to Greece, but he returned to England during the summer of 1639 because of what he claimed in <i>Defensio Secunda</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> were "sad tidings of civil war in England."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Matters became more complicated when Milton received word that his childhood friend Diodati had died. Milton in fact stayed another seven months on the continent, and spent time at <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a> with Diodati\'s uncle after he returned to Rome. In <i>Defensio Secunda</i>, Milton proclaimed that he was warned against a return to Rome because of his frankness about religion, but he stayed in the city for two months and was able to experience <a href="/wiki/Carnival" title="Carnival">Carnival</a> and meet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lukas_Holste" title="Lukas Holste">Lukas Holste</a>, a Vatican librarian who guided Milton through its collection. He was introduced to Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Barberini_(1597%E2%80%931679)" title="Francesco Barberini (1597&#8211;1679)">Francesco Barberini</a> who invited Milton to an opera hosted by the Cardinal. Around March, Milton travelled once again to Florence, staying there for two months, attending further meetings of the academies, and spending time with friends. After leaving Florence, he travelled through Lucca, Bologna, and Ferrara before coming to <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>. In Venice, Milton was exposed to a model of Republicanism, later important in his political writings, but he soon found another model when he travelled to Geneva. From Switzerland, Milton travelled to Paris and then to Calais before finally arriving back in England in either July or August 1639.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Civil_war.2C_prose_tracts.2C_and_marriage"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war,_prose_tracts,_and_marriage">Civil war, prose tracts, and marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Milton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Civil war, prose tracts, and marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Milton%27s_antiprelatical_tracts" title="Milton\'s antiprelatical tracts">Milton\'s antiprelatical tracts</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Areopagitica_bridwell.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="561" data-file-width="399" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ed/Areopagitica_bridwell.jpg/200px-Areopagitica_bridwell.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ed/Areopagitica_bridwell.jpg/300px-Areopagitica_bridwell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/ed/Areopagitica_bridwell.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Areopagitica_bridwell.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of the 1644 edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Areopagitica" title="Areopagitica">Areopagitica</a></i></div></div></div>\n<p>On returning to England where the <a href="/wiki/Bishops%27_Wars" title="Bishops\' Wars">Bishops\' Wars</a> presaged further armed conflict, Milton began to write prose <a href="/wiki/Tract_(literature)" title="Tract (literature)">tracts</a> against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Episcopacy" title="Episcopacy">episcopacy</a>, in the service of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puritan" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Long_Parliament" title="Long Parliament">Parliamentary</a> cause. Milton\'s first foray into polemics was <i>Of Reformation touching Church Discipline in England</i> (1641), followed by <i>Of Prelatical Episcopacy</i>, the two defences of <a href="/wiki/Smectymnuus" title="Smectymnuus">Smectymnuus</a> (a group of Presbyterian divines named from their initials; the "TY" belonged to Milton\'s old tutor Thomas Young), and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Reason_of_Church-Government_Urged_against_Prelaty" title="The Reason of Church-Government Urged against Prelaty">The Reason of Church-Government Urged against Prelaty</a></i>. He vigorously attacked the High-church party of the Church of England and their leader <a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">William Laud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, with frequent passages of real eloquence lighting up the rough controversial style of the period, and deploying a wide knowledge of church history.\n</p><p>He was supported by his father\'s investments, but Milton became a private schoolmaster at this time, educating his nephews and other children of the well-to-do. This experience and discussions with educational reformer <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Hartlib" title="Samuel Hartlib">Samuel Hartlib</a> led him to write his short tract <i><a href="/wiki/Of_Education" title="Of Education">Of Education</a></i> in 1644, urging a reform of the national universities.\n</p><p>In June 1642, Milton paid a visit to the manor house at <a href="/wiki/Forest_Hill,_Oxfordshire" title="Forest Hill, Oxfordshire">Forest Hill, Oxfordshire</a>, and returned with 16-year-old bride Mary Powell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_Milton_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB_Milton-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Mary found life difficult with the severe 35-year-old schoolmaster and pamphleteer, and she returned to her family a month later. She did not return until 1645, partly because of the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/First_English_Civil_War" title="First English Civil War">Civil War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODNB_Milton_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB_Milton-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the meantime, her desertion prompted Milton to publish <a href="/wiki/Milton%27s_divorce_tracts" title="Milton\'s divorce tracts">a series of pamphlets</a> over the next three years arguing for the legality and morality of divorce. (<a href="/wiki/Anna_Beer" title="Anna Beer">Anna Beer</a>, one of Milton\'s most recent biographers, points to a lack of evidence and the dangers of cynicism in urging that it was not necessarily the case that the private life so animated the public polemicising.) In 1643, Milton had a brush with the authorities over these writings, in parallel with <a href="/wiki/Hezekiah_Woodward" title="Hezekiah Woodward">Hezekiah Woodward</a>, who had more trouble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> It was the hostile response accorded the divorce tracts that spurred Milton to write <i><a href="/wiki/Areopagitica" title="Areopagitica">Areopagitica; A speech of Mr. John Milton for the Liberty of Unlicenc\'d Printing, to the Parlament of England</a></i>, his celebrated attack on pre-printing censorship.  In <i>Areopagitica</i>, Milton aligns himself with the parliamentary cause, and he also begins to synthesize the ideal of neo-Roman liberty with that of Christian liberty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_for_Foreign_Tongues">Secretary for Foreign Tongues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Milton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Secretary for Foreign Tongues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>With the parliamentary victory in the Civil War, Milton used his pen in defence of the republican principles represented by the Commonwealth. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tenure_of_Kings_and_Magistrates" title="The Tenure of Kings and Magistrates">The Tenure of Kings and Magistrates</a></i> (1649) defended the right of the people to hold their rulers to account, and implicitly sanctioned the <a href="/wiki/Regicide" title="Regicide">regicide</a>; Milton\'s political reputation got him appointed Secretary for Foreign Tongues by the Council of State in March 1649. His main job description was to compose the English Republic\'s foreign correspondence in Latin, but he also was called upon to produce propaganda for the regime and to serve as a censor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:19_York_Street,_Westminster_(1848).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="371" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/19_York_Street%2C_Westminster_%281848%29.jpg/220px-19_York_Street%2C_Westminster_%281848%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/19_York_Street%2C_Westminster_%281848%29.jpg/330px-19_York_Street%2C_Westminster_%281848%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/61/19_York_Street%2C_Westminster_%281848%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:19_York_Street,_Westminster_(1848).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The back of no 19 York Street (1848). In 1651, Milton moved into a "pretty garden-house" in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petty_France,_Westminster" title="Petty France, Westminster">Petty France, Westminster</a>. He lived there until the Restoration. Later it became No. 19 York Street, belonged to <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a>, was occupied successively by <a href="/wiki/James_Mill" title="James Mill">James Mill</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Hazlitt" title="William Hazlitt">William Hazlitt</a>, and finally was demolished in 1877.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In October 1649, he published <i><a href="/wiki/Eikonoklastes" title="Eikonoklastes">Eikonoklastes</a></i>, an explicit defence of the regicide, in response to the <i><a href="/wiki/Eikon_Basilike" title="Eikon Basilike">Eikon Basilike</a></i>, a phenomenal best-seller popularly attributed to Charles I that portrayed the King as an innocent Christian <a href="/wiki/Martyr" title="Martyr">martyr</a>. Milton tried to break this powerful image of Charles I (the literal translation of Eikonoklastes is \'the image breaker\'). A month later, however, the exiled <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a> and his party published the defence of monarchy <i>Defensio Regia pro Carolo Primo</i>, written by leading humanist <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Salmasius" title="Claudius Salmasius">Claudius Salmasius</a>. By January of the following year, Milton was ordered to write a defence of the English people by the <a href="/wiki/English_Council_of_State" title="English Council of State">Council of State</a>. Milton worked more slowly than usual, given the European audience and the English Republic\'s desire to establish diplomatic and cultural legitimacy, as he drew on the learning marshalled by his years of study to compose a riposte.\n</p><p>On 24 February 1652, Milton published his Latin defence of the English people <i><a href="/wiki/Defensio_pro_Populo_Anglicano" title="Defensio pro Populo Anglicano">Defensio pro Populo Anglicano</a></i>, also known as the <i>First Defence</i>. Milton\'s pure Latin prose and evident learning exemplified in the <i>First Defence</i> quickly made him a European reputation, and the work ran to numerous editions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> He addressed his <i>Sonnet 16</i> to \'The Lord Generall Cromwell in May 1652\' beginning  "Cromwell, our chief of men...", although it was not published until 1654.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1654, Milton completed the second defence of the English nation <i>Defensio secunda</i> in response to an anonymous Royalist tract <i>"Regii Sanguinis Clamor ad Coelum Adversus Parricidas Anglicanos"</i> [The Cry of the Royal Blood to Heaven  Against the English Parricides], a work that made many personal attacks on Milton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The second defence praised <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>, now Lord Protector, while exhorting him to remain true to the principles of the Revolution. <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Morus" title="Alexander Morus">Alexander Morus</a>, to whom Milton wrongly attributed the <i>Clamor</i> (in fact by <a href="/wiki/Peter_du_Moulin" title="Peter du Moulin">Peter du Moulin</a>), published an attack on Milton, in response to which Milton published the autobiographical <i>Defensio pro se</i> in 1655. Milton held the appointment of Secretary for Foreign Tongues to the Commonwealth Council of State until 1660, although after he had become totally blind, most of the work was done by his deputies, Georg Rudolph Wecklein, then Philip Meadows, and from 1657 by the poet <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Marvell" title="Andrew Marvell">Andrew Marvell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1654, Milton had become totally blind; the cause of his blindness is debated but bilateral <a href="/wiki/Retinal_detachment" title="Retinal detachment">retinal detachment</a> or <a href="/wiki/Glaucoma" title="Glaucoma">glaucoma</a> are most likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18168884_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18168884-40">[40]</a></sup> His blindness forced him to dictate his <a href="/wiki/Verse_(poetry)" title="Verse (poetry)">verse</a> and prose to <a href="/wiki/Amanuensis" title="Amanuensis">amanuenses</a> who copied them out for him; one of these was poet, Andrew Marvell. One of his best-known sonnets is presumed to date from this period, <i><a href="/wiki/When_I_Consider_How_My_Light_is_Spent" title="When I Consider How My Light is Spent">When I Consider How My Light is Spent</a></i>, titled by a later editor, Bishop John Newton, "<i>On His Blindness</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Restoration">The Restoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Milton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Restoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Cromwell\'s death in 1658 caused the English Republic to collapse into feuding military and political factions. Milton, however, stubbornly clung to the beliefs that had originally inspired him to write for the Commonwealth. In 1659, he published <i><a href="/wiki/A_Treatise_of_Civil_Power" title="A Treatise of Civil Power">A Treatise of Civil Power</a></i>, attacking the concept of a state-dominated church (the position known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erastianism" title="Erastianism">Erastianism</a>), as well as <i>Considerations touching the likeliest means to remove hirelings</i>, denouncing corrupt practises in church governance. As the Republic disintegrated, Milton wrote several proposals to retain a non-monarchical government against the wishes of parliament, soldiers, and the people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>A Letter to a Friend, Concerning the Ruptures of the Commonwealth</i>, written in October 1659, was a response to <a href="/wiki/John_Lambert_(general)" title="John Lambert (general)">General Lambert</a>\'s recent dissolution of the <a href="/wiki/Rump_Parliament" title="Rump Parliament">Rump Parliament</a>.</li>\n<li><i>Proposals of certain expedients for the preventing of a civil war now feared</i>, written in November 1659.</li>\n<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Ready_and_Easy_Way_to_Establishing_a_Free_Commonwealth" title="The Ready and Easy Way to Establishing a Free Commonwealth">The Ready and Easy Way to Establishing a Free Commonwealth</a></i>, in two editions, responded to <a href="/wiki/George_Monck,_1st_Duke_of_Albemarle" title="George Monck, 1st Duke of Albemarle">General Monck</a>\'s march towards London to restore the <a href="/wiki/Long_Parliament" title="Long Parliament">Long Parliament</a> (which led to the restoration of the monarchy). The work is an impassioned, bitter, and futile <a href="/wiki/Jeremiad" title="Jeremiad">jeremiad</a> damning the English people for backsliding from the cause of <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">liberty</a> and advocating the establishment of an authoritarian rule by an <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a> set up by unelected parliament.</li></ul>\n<p>Upon the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Restoration" title="English Restoration">Restoration</a> in May 1660, Milton went into hiding for his life, while a warrant was issued for his arrest and his writings were burnt. He re-emerged after <a href="/wiki/Indemnity_and_Oblivion_Act" title="Indemnity and Oblivion Act">a general pardon</a> was issued, but was nevertheless arrested and briefly imprisoned before influential friends intervened, such as Marvell, now an MP. Milton married for a third and final time on 24 February 1663, marrying Elizabeth (Betty) Minshull, aged 24, a native of <a href="/wiki/Wistaston" title="Wistaston">Wistaston</a>, Cheshire. He spent the remaining decade of his life living quietly in London, only retiring to a cottage during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plague_of_London" title="Great Plague of London">Great Plague of London</a>&#8212;<a href="/wiki/Milton%27s_Cottage" title="Milton\'s Cottage">Milton\'s Cottage</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chalfont_St._Giles" title="Chalfont St. Giles">Chalfont St. Giles</a>, his only extant home.\n</p><p>During this period, Milton published several minor prose works, such as the grammar textbook <i>Art of Logic</i> and a <i>History of Britain</i>. His only explicitly political tracts were the 1672 <i>Of True Religion</i>, arguing for <a href="/wiki/Toleration" title="Toleration">toleration</a> (except for Catholics), and a translation of a Polish tract advocating an elective monarchy. Both these works were referred to in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exclusion_Bill" title="Exclusion Bill">Exclusion</a> debate, the attempt to exclude the heir presumptive from the throne of England&#8212;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James,_Duke_of_York" title="James, Duke of York">James, Duke of York</a>&#8212;because he was Roman Catholic. That debate preoccupied politics in the 1670s and 1680s and precipitated the formation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Whig_Party" title="British Whig Party">Whig party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>.\n</p><p>Milton died of kidney failure on 8 November 1674 and was buried in the church of <a href="/wiki/St_Giles-without-Cripplegate" title="St Giles-without-Cripplegate">St Giles-without-Cripplegate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fore_Street,_London" title="Fore Street, London">Fore Street</a>, London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncylLon_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncylLon-44">[44]</a></sup> According to an early biographer, his funeral was attended by "his learned and great Friends in London, not without a friendly concourse of the Vulgar."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> A monument was added in 1793, sculpted by <a href="/wiki/John_Bacon_(sculptor)" title="John Bacon (sculptor)">John Bacon the Elder</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Milton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Milton and his first wife Mary Powell (1625&#8211;1652) had four children:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Anne (born 7 July 1646)</li>\n<li>Mary (born 25 October 1648)</li>\n<li>John (16 March 1651 &#8211; June 1652)</li>\n<li>Deborah (2 May 1652 &#8211; 10 August 1727<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>)</li></ul>\n<p>Mary Powell died on 5 May 1652 from complications following Deborah\'s birth. Milton\'s daughters survived to adulthood, but he always had a strained relationship with them.\n</p><p>On 12 November 1656, Milton was married to Katherine Woodcock at <a href="/wiki/St_Margaret%27s,_Westminster" title="St Margaret\'s, Westminster">St Margaret\'s, Westminster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> She died on 3 February 1658, less than four months after giving birth to her daughter Katherine, who also died.\n</p><p>Milton married for a third time on 24 February 1663 to Elizabeth Mynshull or Minshull (1638&#8211;1728), the niece of Thomas Mynshull, a wealthy apothecary and philanthropist in Manchester. The marriage took place at <a href="/wiki/St_Mary_Aldermary" title="St Mary Aldermary">St Mary Aldermary</a> in the City of London. Despite a 31-year age gap, the marriage seemed happy, according to <a href="/wiki/John_Aubrey" title="John Aubrey">John Aubrey</a>, and lasted more than 12 years until Milton\'s death. (A plaque on the wall of Mynshull\'s House in Manchester describes Elizabeth as Milton\'s "3rd and Best wife".) Samuel Johnson, however, claims that Mynshull was "a domestic companion and attendant" and that Milton\'s nephew <a href="/wiki/Edward_Phillips" title="Edward Phillips">Edward Phillips</a> relates that Mynshull "oppressed his children in his lifetime, and cheated them at his death".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>His nephews, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Phillips" title="Edward Phillips">Edward</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Phillips_(author)" title="John Phillips (author)">John Phillips</a> (sons of Milton\'s sister Anne), were educated by Milton and became writers themselves. John acted as a secretary, and Edward was Milton\'s first biographer.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Poetry">Poetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Milton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Poetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Milton\'s poetry was slow to see the light of day, at least under his name. His first published poem was "On Shakespeare" (1630), anonymously included in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Folio" title="Second Folio">Second Folio</a> edition of <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>\'s plays in 1632. Milton collected his work in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1645_Poems" title="1645 Poems">1645 Poems</a></i> in the midst of the excitement attending the possibility of establishing a new English government. The anonymous edition of <i>Comus</i> was published in 1637, and the publication of <i>Lycidas</i> in 1638 in <i>Justa Edouardo King Naufrago</i> was signed J. M. Otherwise. The 1645 collection was the only poetry of his to see print until <i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost">Paradise Lost</a></i> appeared in 1667.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paradise_Lost"><i>Paradise Lost</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Milton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Paradise Lost">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost">Paradise Lost</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_032.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1170" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_032.jpg/220px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_032.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_032.jpg/330px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_032.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_032.jpg/440px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_032.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_032.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Milton Dictates the Lost Paradise to His Three Daughters</i>, ca. 1826, by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eug&#232;ne Delacroix">Eug&#232;ne Delacroix</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Milton\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Masterpiece" title="Masterpiece">magnum opus</a></i>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blank-verse" title="Blank-verse">blank-verse</a> <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic poem</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost">Paradise Lost</a></i>, was composed by the blind and impoverished Milton from 1658 to 1664 (first edition), with small but significant revisions published in 1674 (second edition). As a blind poet, Milton dictated his verse to a series of aides in his employ. It has been argued that the poem reflects his personal despair at the failure of the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">Revolution</a>, yet affirms an ultimate optimism in human potential. Some literary critics have argued that Milton encoded many references to his unyielding support for the "<a href="/wiki/Good_Old_Cause" title="Good Old Cause">Good Old Cause</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 27 April 1667,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindenbaum1995_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lindenbaum1995-50">[50]</a></sup> Milton sold the publication rights for <i>Paradise Lost</i> to publisher <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Simmons" title="Samuel Simmons">Samuel Simmons</a> for &#163;5 (equivalent to approximately &#163;770 in 2015 purchasing power),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> with a further &#163;5 to be paid if and when each print run sold out of between 1,300 and 1,500 copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The first run was a <a href="/wiki/Bookbinding" title="Bookbinding">quarto</a> edition priced at three <a href="/wiki/Shilling_(English_coin)" title="Shilling (English coin)">shillings</a> per copy (about &#163;23 in 2015 purchasing power equivalent), published in August 1667, and it sold out in eighteen months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Milton followed up the publication <i>Paradise Lost</i> with its sequel <i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Regained" title="Paradise Regained">Paradise Regained</a></i>, which was published alongside the tragedy <i><a href="/wiki/Samson_Agonistes" title="Samson Agonistes">Samson Agonistes</a></i> in 1671. Both of these works also resonate with Milton\'s post-Restoration political situation. Just before his death in 1674, Milton supervised a second edition of <i>Paradise Lost</i>, accompanied by an explanation of "why the poem rhymes not", and prefatory verses by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Marvell" title="Andrew Marvell">Andrew Marvell</a>. In 1673, Milton republished his <i>1645 Poems</i>, as well as a collection of his letters and the Latin prolusions from his Oxford days.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Views">Views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Milton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>An unfinished religious manifesto, <i><a href="/wiki/De_Doctrina_Christiana_(Milton)" title="De Doctrina Christiana (Milton)">De doctrina christiana</a></i>, probably written by Milton, lays out many of his heterodox theological views, and was not discovered and published until 1823. Milton\'s key beliefs were idiosyncratic, not those of an identifiable group or faction, and often they go well beyond the orthodoxy of the time. Their tone, however, stemmed from the Puritan emphasis on the centrality and inviolability of conscience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> He was his own man, but he was anticipated by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Robinson_(writer)" title="Henry Robinson (writer)">Henry Robinson</a> in <i>Areopagitica</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Milton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>By the late 1650s, Milton was a proponent of <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monism</a> or animist materialism, the notion that a single material substance which is "animate, self-active, and free" composes everything in the universe: from stones and trees and bodies to minds, souls, angels, and God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Milton devised this position to avoid the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">mind-body dualism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">Descartes</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_(philosophy)" title="Mechanism (philosophy)">mechanistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determinism</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hobbes" title="Hobbes">Hobbes</a>. Milton\'s monism is most notably reflected in <i>Paradise Lost</i> when he has angels eat (5.433&#8211;39)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and engage in sexual intercourse (8.622&#8211;29)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and the <i>De Doctrina</i>, where he denies the dual natures of man and argues for a theory of Creation <a href="/wiki/Ex_nihilo" title="Ex nihilo"><i>ex Deo</i></a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_thought">Political thought</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Milton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Political thought">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/John_Milton%27s_politics" title="John Milton\'s politics">John Milton\'s politics</a></div><p>Milton was a "passionately individual Christian Humanist poet."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-56">[56]</a></sup> He appears on the pages of seventeenth century English Puritanism, an age characterized as "the world turned upside down."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> He is a Puritan and yet is unwilling to surrender conscience to party positions on public policy. Thus, Milton\'s political thought, driven by competing convictions, a Reformed faith and a Humanist spirit, led to enigmatic outcomes.</p><blockquote><p>Milton&#8217;s apparently contradictory stance on the vital problems of his age, arose from religious contestations, to the questions of the divine rights of kings. In both the cases, he seems in control, taking stock of the situation arising from the polarization of the English society on religious and political lines. He fought with the Puritans against the Cavaliers i.e. the King&#8217;s party, and helped win the day. But the very same constitutional and republican polity, when tried to curtail freedom of speech, Milton, given his humanistic zeal, wrote Areopagitica . . .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-58">[58]</a></sup></p></blockquote><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Areopagitica_1644bw_gobeirne.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Areopagitica_1644bw_gobeirne.png/170px-Areopagitica_1644bw_gobeirne.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Areopagitica_1644bw_gobeirne.png/255px-Areopagitica_1644bw_gobeirne.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Areopagitica_1644bw_gobeirne.png/340px-Areopagitica_1644bw_gobeirne.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Areopagitica_1644bw_gobeirne.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of John Milton\'s 1644 edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Areopagitica" title="Areopagitica">Areopagitica</a></i></div></div></div>\n<p>Milton\'s political thought may be best categorized according to respective periods in his life and times. The years 1641&#8211;42 were dedicated to church politics and the struggle against episcopacy. After his divorce writings, <i>Areopagitica</i>, and a gap, he wrote in 1649&#8211;54 in the aftermath of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Execution_of_Charles_I" title="Execution of Charles I">execution of Charles I</a>, and in polemic justification of the regicide and the existing Parliamentarian regime. Then in 1659&#8211;60 he foresaw the Restoration, and wrote to head it off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Milton\'s own beliefs were in some cases both unpopular and dangerous, and this was true particularly to his commitment to <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a>. In coming centuries, Milton would be claimed as an early apostle of liberalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> According to James Tully:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>... with <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">Locke</a> as with Milton, republican and <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">contraction</a> conceptions of political freedom join hands in common opposition to the disengaged and passive subjection offered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolutism_(European_history)" title="Absolutism (European history)">absolutists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Hobbes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Filmer" title="Robert Filmer">Robert Filmer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>A friend and ally in the pamphlet wars was <a href="/wiki/Marchamont_Nedham" title="Marchamont Nedham">Marchamont Nedham</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austin_Woolrych" title="Austin Woolrych">Austin Woolrych</a> considers that although they were quite close, there is "little real affinity, beyond a broad republicanism", between their approaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Blair_Worden" title="Blair Worden">Blair Worden</a> remarks that both Milton and Nedham, with others such as <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Marvell" title="Andrew Marvell">Andrew Marvell</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Harrington_(author)" title="James Harrington (author)">James Harrington</a>, would have taken their problem with the <a href="/wiki/Rump_Parliament" title="Rump Parliament">Rump Parliament</a> to be not the republic itself, but the fact that it was not a proper republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Woolrych speaks of "the gulf between Milton\'s vision of the Commonwealth\'s future and the reality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In the early version of his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Britain_(John_Milton)" title="History of Britain (John Milton)">History of Britain</a></i>, begun in 1649, Milton was already writing off the members of the <a href="/wiki/Long_Parliament" title="Long Parliament">Long Parliament</a> as incorrigible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He praised <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> as the Protectorate was set up; though subsequently he had major reservations. When Cromwell seemed to be backsliding as a revolutionary, after a couple of years in power, Milton moved closer to the position of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Vane_the_Younger" title="Henry Vane the Younger">Sir Henry Vane</a>, to whom he wrote a sonnet in 1652.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The group of disaffected republicans included, besides Vane, <a href="/wiki/John_Bradshaw_(judge)" title="John Bradshaw (judge)">John Bradshaw</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Hutchinson_(Colonel)" title="John Hutchinson (Colonel)">John Hutchinson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Ludlow" title="Edmund Ludlow">Edmund Ludlow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Marten_(regicide)" title="Henry Marten (regicide)">Henry Marten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Overton" title="Robert Overton">Robert Overton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Sexby" title="Edward Sexby">Edward Sexby</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Streater" title="John Streater">John Streater</a>; but not Marvell, who remained with Cromwell\'s party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Milton had already commended Overton, along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edmund_Whalley" title="Edmund Whalley">Edmund Whalley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulstrode_Whitelocke" title="Bulstrode Whitelocke">Bulstrode Whitelocke</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/Defensio_Secunda" title="Defensio Secunda">Defensio Secunda</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Nigel Smith writes that\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>... John Streater, and the form of republicanism he stood for, was a fulfilment of Milton\'s most optimistic ideas of free speech and of public heroism [...]<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>As <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cromwell" title="Richard Cromwell">Richard Cromwell</a> fell from power, he envisaged a step towards a freer republic or "free commonwealth", writing in the hope of this outcome in early 1660. Milton had argued for an awkward position, in the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ready_and_Easy_Way_to_Establish_a_Free_Commonwealth" title="The Ready and Easy Way to Establish a Free Commonwealth">Ready and Easy Way</a></i>, because he wanted to invoke the <a href="/wiki/Good_Old_Cause" title="Good Old Cause">Good Old Cause</a> and gain the support of the republicans, but without offering a democratic solution of any kind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> His proposal, backed by reference (amongst other reasons) to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oligarchical" title="Oligarchical">oligarchical</a> Dutch and Venetian constitutions, was for a council with perpetual membership. This attitude cut right across the grain of popular opinion of the time, which swung decisively behind the restoration of the Stuart monarchy that took place later in the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Milton, an associate of and advocate on behalf of the regicides, was silenced on political matters as Charles II returned.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Milton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Milton%27s_religion" title="John Milton\'s religion">John Milton\'s religion</a></div>\n<p>Milton was not a clergyman. He was not a theologian. However, theology, particularly English Calvinism, formed the great palette on which John Milton created his greatest thoughts.  John Milton wrestled with the great doctrines of the Church amidst the theological crosswinds of his age. The great Poet was undoubtedly Reformed (though his grandfather, Richard "the Ranger" Milton had been Roman Catholic).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-5">[5]</a></sup> However, Milton\'s Calvinism had to find expression in a broad-spirited Humanism. Like many <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> artists before him, Milton attempted to integrate Christian theology with classical modes. In his early poems, the poet narrator expresses a tension between vice and virtue, the latter invariably related to Protestantism. In <i>Comus</i>, Milton may make ironic use of the <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Caroline</a> court <a href="/wiki/Masque" title="Masque">masque</a> by elevating notions of purity and virtue over the conventions of court revelry and superstition. In his later poems, Milton\'s theological concerns become more explicit.\n</p><p>His use of biblical citation was wide-ranging; Harris Fletcher, standing at the beginning of the intensification of the study of the use of scripture in Milton\'s work (poetry and prose, in all languages Milton mastered), notes that typically Milton clipped and adapted biblical quotations to suit the purpose, giving precise chapter and verse only in texts for a more specialized readership. As for the plenitude of Milton\'s quotations from scripture, Fletcher comments, "For this work, I have in all actually collated about twenty-five hundred of the five to ten thousand direct Biblical quotations which appear therein".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harris_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-harris-74">[74]</a></sup> Milton\'s customary English Bible was the <a href="/wiki/King_James_Version#Authorized_Version" title="King James Version">Authorized King James</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> When citing and writing in other languages, he usually employed the Latin translation by <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Tremellius" title="Immanuel Tremellius">Immanuel Tremellius</a>, though "he was equipped to read the Bible in Latin, in Greek, and in Hebrew, including the Targumim or Aramaic paraphrases of the Old Testament, and the Syriac version of the New, together with the available commentaries of those several versions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harris_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-harris-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Milton embraced many heterodox Christian theological views. He has been accused of rejecting the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>, believing instead that the Son was subordinate to the Father, a position known as <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arianism</a>; and his sympathy or curiosity was probably engaged by <a href="/wiki/Socinianism" title="Socinianism">Socinianism</a>: in August 1650 he licensed for publication by <a href="/wiki/William_Dugard" title="William Dugard">William Dugard</a> the <i><a href="/wiki/Racovian_Catechism" title="Racovian Catechism">Racovian Catechism</a></i>, based on a non-trinitarian creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Milton\'s alleged Arianism, like much of his theology, is still subject of debate and controversy. <a href="/wiki/Rufus_Wilmot_Griswold" title="Rufus Wilmot Griswold">Rufus Wilmot Griswold</a> argued that "In none of his great works is there a passage from which it can be inferred that he was an Arian; and in the very last of his writings he declares that "the doctrine of the Trinity is a plain doctrine in Scripture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> In <i>Areopagitica,</i> Milton classified Arians and Socinians as "errorists" and "schismatics" alongside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arminians" title="Arminians">Arminians</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptists" title="Anabaptists">Anabaptists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> A source has interpreted him as broadly Protestant, if not always easy to locate in a more precise religious category.\n</p><p>In his 1641 treatise, <i><a href="/wiki/Of_Reformation" title="Of Reformation">Of Reformation</a></i>, Milton expressed his dislike for Catholicism and episcopacy, presenting Rome as a modern <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, and bishops as Egyptian taskmasters. These analogies conform to Milton\'s puritanical preference for <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> imagery. He knew at least four commentaries on <i>Genesis</i>: those of <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paulus_Fagius" title="Paulus Fagius">Paulus Fagius</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Pareus" title="David Pareus">David Pareus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andreus_Rivetus" title="Andreus Rivetus">Andreus Rivetus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Through the <a href="/wiki/Interregnum_(1649%E2%80%931660)" title="Interregnum (1649&#8211;1660)">Interregnum</a>, Milton often presents England, rescued from the trappings of a worldly monarchy, as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elect" title="Elect">elect</a> nation akin to the Old Testament <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, and shows its leader, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>, as a latter-day <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>. These views were bound up in Protestant views of the <a href="/wiki/Millennium" title="Millennium">Millennium</a>, which some sects, such as the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Monarchists" title="Fifth Monarchists">Fifth Monarchists</a> predicted would arrive in England. Milton, however, would later criticise the "worldly" millenarian views of these and others, and expressed orthodox ideas on the prophecy of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_Empires" title="Four Empires">Four Empires</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Restoration of the Stuart monarchy in 1660 began a new phase in Milton\'s work. In <i>Paradise Lost</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Regained" title="Paradise Regained">Paradise Regained</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Samson_Agonistes" title="Samson Agonistes">Samson Agonistes</a></i>, Milton mourns the end of the godly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Commonwealth" title="English Commonwealth">Commonwealth</a>.
